<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore,_Maryland&amp;redirect=no" title="Baltimore, Maryland">Baltimore, Maryland</a>)</span></div>

For the surrounding county, see <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_County,_Maryland" title="Baltimore County, Maryland">Baltimore County, Maryland</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Baltimore_(disambiguation)" title="Baltimore (disambiguation)">Baltimore (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<td><a class="external text" href="http://www.baltimorecity.gov/" rel="nofollow">City of Baltimore</a></td>

<p><b>Baltimore</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɔːr/: 'ar' in 'war'">ɔːr</span></span>, -<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɔər/: 'ore' in 'wore'">ɔər</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <small>locally:</small> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[ˈbɔɫ.mɔɻ]</a></span>) is the largest city in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_population" title="List of United States cities by population">29th-most populous city</a> in the country.</p>
<p>Baltimore was established by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Maryland" title="Constitution of Maryland">Constitution of Maryland</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and is not part of any county. With a population of 621,849 in 2015, Baltimore is the largest <a href="/wiki/Independent_city_(United_States)" title="Independent city (United States)">independent city</a> in the United States. As of 2016, the population of the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_metropolitan_area" title="Baltimore metropolitan area">Baltimore metropolitan area</a> was estimated to be just under 2.8 million, making it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_of_the_United_States" title="List of metropolitan areas of the United States">21st largest metropolitan area</a> in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopEstCBSA_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-PopEstCBSA-10">[10]</a></sup> Baltimore is also part of the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_metropolitan_area" title="Baltimore–Washington metropolitan area">Washington-Baltimore combined statistical area (CSA)</a>, the fourth largest CSA in the nation with a calculated 2016 population of 9,665,892.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Founded in 1729, Baltimore is the second-largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaport" title="Seaport">seaport</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_states" title="Mid-Atlantic states">Mid-Atlantic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-12">[12]</a></sup> The city's <a href="/wiki/Inner_Harbor" title="Inner Harbor">Inner Harbor</a> was once the second leading port of entry for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immigrant" title="Immigrant">immigrants</a> to the United States and a major manufacturing center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> After a decline in major manufacturing, industrialization, and rail transportation, Baltimore shifted to a <a href="/wiki/Service_economy" title="Service economy">service-oriented economy</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Hospital" title="Johns Hopkins Hospital">Johns Hopkins Hospital</a> (founded 1889) and <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">Johns Hopkins University</a> (founded 1876), now the city's top two employers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>With hundreds of identified districts, Baltimore has been dubbed a "city of neighborhoods." Famous residents have included writers <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edith_Hamilton" title="Edith Hamilton">Edith Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.L._Mencken" title="H.L. Mencken">H.L. Mencken</a>; jazz musician <a href="/wiki/Eubie_Blake" title="Eubie Blake">James "Eubie" Blake</a>; singer <a href="/wiki/Billie_Holiday" title="Billie Holiday">Billie Holiday</a>; actor and filmmaker <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Waters_(filmmaker)" title="John Waters (filmmaker)">John Waters</a>; and baseball player <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Scott_Key" title="Francis Scott Key">Francis Scott Key</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">The Star-Spangled Banner</a></i>, which later became the American national anthem, in Baltimore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baltimore.org_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-baltimore.org-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore has more public statues and monuments per capita than any other city in the country,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and is home to some of the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Register_Historic_District" title="National Register Historic District">National Register Historic Districts</a> in the nation, including <a href="/wiki/Fell%27s_Point,_Baltimore" title="Fell's Point, Baltimore">Fell's Point</a>, <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hill,_Baltimore" title="Federal Hill, Baltimore">Federal Hill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vernon,_Baltimore" title="Mount Vernon, Baltimore">Mount Vernon</a>, which were added to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Register" title="National Register">National Register</a> between 1969–1971. Close to a third of the city's buildings (over 65,000) are designated as historic in the National Register, which is more than any other U.S. city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Baltimore" title="History of Baltimore">History of Baltimore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Baltimore" title="Timeline of Baltimore">Timeline of Baltimore</a></div>
<p>The city has 66 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Register_Historic_District" title="National Register Historic District">National Register Historic Districts</a> and 33 local historic districts. Over 65,000 properties are designated historic buildings in the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places_listings_in_Baltimore" title="National Register of Historic Places listings in Baltimore">National Register of Historic Places listings in Baltimore</a>, more than any other U.S. city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historical records of the government of Baltimore are located at the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_Archives" title="Baltimore City Archives">Baltimore City Archives</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city is named after <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Calvert,_2nd_Baron_Baltimore" title="Cecil Calvert, 2nd Baron Baltimore">Cecil Calvert, second Lord Baltimore</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> (1605–1675),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_House_of_Lords" title="Irish House of Lords">Irish House of Lords</a> and founding proprietor of the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Maryland" title="Province of Maryland">Province of Maryland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egan_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-egan-22">[22]</a></sup> <i>Baltimore Manor</i> was the name of the estate in <a href="/wiki/County_Longford" title="County Longford">County Longford</a> on which the Calvert family lived in Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egan_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-egan-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Baltimore is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicization" title="Anglicization">anglicization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> name <i>Baile an Tí Mhóir</i>, meaning "town of the big house."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egan_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-egan-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_European_settlement">Before European settlement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Before European settlement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Baltimore area had been inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> since at least the <a href="/wiki/10th_millennium_BC" title="10th millennium BC">10th millennium BC</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indians</a> first settled in the region. One Paleo-Indian site and several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_period_in_North_America" title="Archaic period in North America">Archaic period</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woodland_period" title="Woodland period">Woodland period</a> archaeological sites have been identified in Baltimore, including four from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Woodland_period" title="Late Woodland period">Late Woodland period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> During the Late Woodland period, the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">archaeological culture</a> that is called the "Potomac Creek complex" resided in the area from Baltimore to the <a href="/wiki/Rappahannock_River" title="Rappahannock River">Rappahannock River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1600s, the immediate Baltimore vicinity was sparsely populated, if at all, by <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a>. The Baltimore County area northward was used as hunting grounds by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Susquehannocks" title="Susquehannocks">Susquehannocks</a> living in the lower <a href="/wiki/Susquehanna_River" title="Susquehanna River">Susquehanna River</a> valley who "controlled all of the upper tributaries of the Chesapeake" but "refrained from much contact with <a href="/wiki/Powhatan" title="Powhatan">Powhatan</a> in the Potomac region."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Pressured by the Susquehannocks, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piscataway_tribe" title="Piscataway tribe">Piscataway tribe</a> of <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_peoples" title="Algonquian peoples">Algonquians</a> stayed well south of the Baltimore area and inhabited primarily the north bank of the <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">Potomac River</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Charles_County,_Maryland" title="Charles County, Maryland">Charles County</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Prince_George%27s_County,_Maryland" title="Prince George's County, Maryland">Prince George's County</a> south of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_Line" title="Fall Line">Fall Line</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_period">Colonial period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonial period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European colonization</a> of Maryland began with the arrival of an English ship at St. Clement's Island in the Potomac River. Europeans began to settle the area further north, beginning to populate the area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore_County" title="Baltimore County">Baltimore County</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrooksRockel1979p1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrooksRockel1979p1-30">[30]</a></sup> with its original <a href="/wiki/County_seat" title="County seat">county seat</a>, known today as "Old Baltimore", located on <a href="/wiki/Bush_River_(Maryland)" title="Bush River (Maryland)">Bush River</a> within the present-day <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen_Proving_Ground" title="Aberdeen Proving Ground">Aberdeen Proving Ground</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RememberingOldBaltimore_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-RememberingOldBaltimore-33">[33]</a></sup> The colonists engaged in sporadic warfare with the Susquhanna natives, whose numbers dwindled from casualties and <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrooksRockel1979p1_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrooksRockel1979p1-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1661 David Jones claimed the area known today as <a href="/wiki/Jonestown,_Baltimore" title="Jonestown, Baltimore">Jonestown</a> on the east bank of the <a href="/wiki/Jones_Falls" title="Jones Falls">Jones Falls</a> stream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>The colonial <a href="/wiki/Maryland_General_Assembly" title="Maryland General Assembly">General Assembly of Maryland</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Baltimore" title="Port of Baltimore">Port of Baltimore</a> at old Whetstone Point (now <a href="/wiki/Locust_Point,_Baltimore" title="Locust Point, Baltimore">Locust Point</a>) in 1706 for the <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_in_the_American_Colonies" title="Tobacco in the American Colonies">tobacco trade</a>. The Town of Baltimore, on the west side of the Jones Falls, was founded and laid out on July 30, 1729; with Jonestown and Fells Point preexisting to the east. The three settlements, covering 60 acres, became a commercial hub and in 1768 became the county seat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltimore_in_1752.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="489" data-file-width="830" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Baltimore_in_1752.png/220px-Baltimore_in_1752.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Baltimore_in_1752.png/330px-Baltimore_in_1752.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Baltimore_in_1752.png/440px-Baltimore_in_1752.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baltimore_in_1752.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Baltimore Town in 1752, (at "The Basin")</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baltimore grew swiftly in the 18th century as a granary for <a href="/wiki/Sugar_plantations_in_the_Caribbean" title="Sugar plantations in the Caribbean">sugar-producing colonies in the Caribbean</a>. The profit from sugar encouraged the cultivation of cane and the importation of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Baltimore established its <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Public_Markets" title="Baltimore Public Markets">public market system</a> in 1763.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Market" title="Lexington Market">Lexington Market</a>, founded in 1782, continues to be known as one of the oldest continuously operating public markets in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Lexington Market was also known to be a place for slave trading, which occurred all over the downtown area and was advertised in the <i>Baltimore Sun</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Baltimore had the first Post Office System in the United States (inaugurated in 1774)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and the first water company chartered in the United States (Baltimore Water Company, 1792).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-firsts_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-firsts-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore played a key part in events leading to and including the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. City leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Plowman_Jr." title="Jonathan Plowman Jr.">Jonathan Plowman Jr.</a> moved the city to join the resistance to British taxes, and merchants signed agreements to not trade with Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> met in the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fite_House" title="Henry Fite House">Henry Fite House</a> from December 1776 to February 1777, effectively making the city the <a href="/wiki/List_of_capitals_in_the_United_States" title="List of capitals in the United States">capital of the United States</a> during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antebellum_period">Antebellum period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Antebellum period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Town of Baltimore, Jonestown, and Fells Point were <a href="/wiki/Municipal_corporation" title="Municipal corporation">incorporated</a> as the <i>City of Baltimore</i> in 1796–1797. The city remained a part of surrounding <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_County,_Maryland" title="Baltimore County, Maryland">Baltimore County</a> and continued to serve as its <a href="/wiki/County_seat" title="County seat">county seat</a> from 1768–1851, after which it became an <a href="/wiki/Independent_city_(United_States)" title="Independent city (United States)">independent city</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maryland_Manual_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maryland_Manual-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="1004" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg/170px-Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg/255px-Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg/340px-Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ft._Henry_bombardement_1814.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bombardment of Fort McHenry by the British. Engraved by John Bower <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baltimore" title="Battle of Baltimore">Battle of Baltimore</a> against the British in 1814 inspired the composition of the USA's national anthem, "<a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">The Star-Spangled Banner</a>," and the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_Monument" title="Battle Monument">Battle Monument</a> which became the city's official emblem. A distinctive local culture started to take shape, and a unique skyline peppered with churches and monuments developed. Baltimore acquired its moniker "The Monumental City" after an 1827 visit to Baltimore by President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>. At an evening function Adams gave the following toast: "Baltimore: the Monumental City—May the days of her safety be as prosperous and happy, as the days of her dangers have been trying and triumphant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-salgaz_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-salgaz-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="403" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg/170px-Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg/255px-Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg/340px-Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Balt_Battle_Monument_1a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Battle_Monument" title="Battle Monument">Battle Monument</a> is the official emblem of the City of Baltimore.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baltimore pioneered the use of <a href="/wiki/Gas_lighting" title="Gas lighting">gas lighting</a> in 1816 and its population grew rapidly in the following decades, with concomitant development of culture and infrastructure. The construction of the federally funded <a href="/wiki/National_Road" title="National Road">National Road</a> (which later became part of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_40" title="U.S. Route 40">U.S. Route 40</a>) and the private <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_and_Ohio_Railroad" title="Baltimore and Ohio Railroad">Baltimore and Ohio Railroad</a> (B. &amp; O.) made Baltimore a major shipping and <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> center by linking the city with major markets in the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwest</a>. By 1820 its population had reached 60,000, and its economy had shifted from its base in tobacco plantations to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sawmilling" title="Sawmilling">sawmilling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a> production. These industries benefited from war but successfully shifted into <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> development during peacetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore suffered one of the worst riots of the antebellum <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a> in 1835, when bad investments led to the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_bank_riot" title="Baltimore bank riot">Baltimore bank riot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Soon after the city created the world's first dental college, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland_School_of_Dentistry" title="University of Maryland School of Dentistry">Baltimore College of Dental Surgery</a>, in 1840, and shared in the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_telegraph_line" title="Baltimore–Washington telegraph line">world's first telegraph line</a>, between Baltimore and Washington DC in 1844.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2166" data-file-width="1678" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg/170px-Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg/255px-Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg/340px-Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harpers_8_11_1877_6th_Regiment_Fighting_Baltimore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sixth Regiment fighting railroad strikers, July 20, 1877<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_after">Civil war and after</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Civil war and after">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Maryland, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_state" title="Slave state">slave state</a> with abundant popular support for <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secession</a> in some areas, remained part of the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, due in part to the Union's strategic occupation of the city in 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Baltimore saw the first casualties of the war on April 19, 1861, when Union Soldiers en route from the <a href="/wiki/President_Street_Station" title="President Street Station">President Street Station</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camden_Yards" title="Camden Yards">Camden Yards</a> clashed with a secessionist mob in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratt_Street_Riot" title="Pratt Street Riot">Pratt Street Riot</a>.</p>
<p>In the midst of the <a href="/wiki/Long_Depression" title="Long Depression">Long Depression</a> which followed the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1873" title="Panic of 1873">Panic of 1873</a>, the Baltimore &amp; Ohio Railroad company attempted to lower its workers' wages, leading to <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_railroad_strike_of_1877" title="Baltimore railroad strike of 1877">strikes and riots</a> in the city and <a href="/wiki/Great_Railroad_Strike_of_1877" title="Great Railroad Strike of 1877">beyond</a>. Strikers clashed with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._National_Guard" title="U.S. National Guard">National Guard</a>, leaving 10 dead and 25 wounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_through_1968">20th century through 1968</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 20th century through 1968">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="359" data-file-width="584" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg/220px-Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg/330px-Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg/440px-Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baltimore_Fire_1904_-_West_from_Pratt_and_Gay_Streets_3a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Great_Baltimore_Fire" title="Great Baltimore Fire">Great Baltimore Fire</a> of 1904, looking west from <a href="/wiki/Pratt_Street" title="Pratt Street">Pratt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gay_Street_(Baltimore)" title="Gay Street (Baltimore)">Gay</a> streets</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On February 7, 1904, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Baltimore_Fire" title="Great Baltimore Fire">Great Baltimore Fire</a> destroyed over 1,500 buildings in 30 hours, leaving more than 70 blocks of the downtown area burned to the ground. Damages were estimated at $150 million—in 1904 dollars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> As the city rebuilt during the next two years, lessons learned from the fire led to improvements in firefighting equipment standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The city grew in area by annexing new suburbs from the surrounding counties through 1918, when the city acquired portions of Baltimore County and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Arundel_County,_Maryland" title="Anne Arundel County, Maryland">Anne Arundel County</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> A state constitutional amendment, approved in 1948, required a special vote of the citizens in any proposed annexation area, effectively preventing any future expansion of the city's boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streetcar" title="Streetcar">Streetcars</a> enabled the development of distant neighborhoods areas such as <a href="/wiki/Edmondson,_Baltimore" title="Edmondson, Baltimore">Edmonson Village</a> whose residents could easily commute to work downtown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Driven by migration from the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">deep South</a> and by <a href="/wiki/White_flight" title="White flight">white suburbanization</a>, the relative size of the city's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">black</a> population grew from 23.8% in 1950 to 46.4% in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Encouraged by real estate <a href="/wiki/Blockbusting" title="Blockbusting">blockbusting</a> techniques, recently settled white areas rapidly became all-black neighborhoods, in a rapid process which was nearly total by 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1968_and_after">1968 and after</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1968 and after">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_riot_of_1968" title="Baltimore riot of 1968">Baltimore riot of 1968</a>, coinciding with <a href="/wiki/King_assassination_riots" title="King assassination riots">riots in other cities</a>, followed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.">assassination of Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> on April 4, 1968. Public order was not restored until April 12, 1968. The Baltimore riot cost the city an estimated $10 million (US$ 69 million in 2017). A total of 11,000 Maryland National Guard and federal troops were ordered into the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Lasting effects of the riot can be seen on the streets of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_1_in_Maryland#Baltimore_City_and_County" title="U.S. Route 1 in Maryland">North Avenue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Howard_Street_(Baltimore)" title="Howard Street (Baltimore)">Howard Street</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gay_Street_(Baltimore)" title="Gay Street (Baltimore)">Gay Street</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Route_140" title="Maryland Route 140">Pennsylvania Avenue</a>, where long stretches of the streets remain barren.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The city experienced challenges again in 1974 when teachers, <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_municipal_strike_of_1974" title="Baltimore municipal strike of 1974">municipal workers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_police_strike" title="Baltimore police strike">police officers</a> conducted strikes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Freddie_Gray" title="Death of Freddie Gray">death of Freddie Gray</a> in April 2015, the city experienced <a href="/wiki/2015_Baltimore_protests" title="2015 Baltimore protests">major protests</a> and international media attention, as well as a clash between local youth and police which resulted in a <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> declaration and curfew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore has suffered from a <a href="/wiki/Crime_in_Baltimore" title="Crime in Baltimore">high homicide rate</a> for several decades, peaking in 1993, and again in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> These deaths have taken a severe toll especially on the local black community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_promotion">Development and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Development and promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the beginning of the 1970s, Baltimore's downtown area known as the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Harbor" title="Inner Harbor">Inner Harbor</a> had been neglected and was occupied by a collection of abandoned warehouses. The nickname "Charm City" came from a 1975 meeting of advertisers seeking to improve the city's reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandler95_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandler95-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandler98_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandler98-70">[70]</a></sup> Efforts to redevelop the area started with the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Science_Center" title="Maryland Science Center">Maryland Science Center</a>, which opened in 1976, the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_World_Trade_Center" title="Baltimore World Trade Center">Baltimore World Trade Center</a> (1977), and the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Convention_Center" title="Baltimore Convention Center">Baltimore Convention Center</a> (1979). <a href="/wiki/Harborplace" title="Harborplace">Harborplace</a>, an urban retail and restaurant complex, opened on the waterfront in 1980, followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aquarium_in_Baltimore" title="National Aquarium in Baltimore">National Aquarium</a>, Maryland's largest tourist destination, and the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Museum_of_Industry" title="Baltimore Museum of Industry">Baltimore Museum of Industry</a> in 1981. During the <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_the_United_States" title="HIV/AIDS in the United States">epidemic of HIV/AIDS in the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_Health_Department" title="Baltimore City Health Department">Baltimore City Health Department</a> official Robert Mehl persuaded the city's mayor to form a committee to address food problems; the Baltimore-based charity <a href="/wiki/Moveable_Feast_(organization)" title="Moveable Feast (organization)">Moveable Feast</a> grew out of this initiative in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuller_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuller-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-godsend_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-godsend-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-73">[73]</a></sup> By 2010, the organization's region of service had expanded from merely Baltimore to include all of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Shore_of_Maryland" title="Eastern Shore of Maryland">Eastern Shore of Maryland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gaypride_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-gaypride-74">[74]</a></sup> In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a> <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">baseball team</a> moved from <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Stadium_(Baltimore)" title="Memorial Stadium (Baltimore)">Memorial Stadium</a> to <a href="/wiki/Oriole_Park_at_Camden_Yards" title="Oriole Park at Camden Yards">Oriole Park at Camden Yards</a>, located downtown near the harbor. <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> held an open-air mass at Camden Yards during his papal visit to the United States in October 1995. Three years later the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Ravens" title="Baltimore Ravens">Baltimore Ravens</a> <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">football team</a> moved into <a href="/wiki/M%26T_Bank_Stadium" title="M&amp;T Bank Stadium">M&amp;T Bank Stadium</a> next to Camden Yards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore has seen the reopening of the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_Theatre_(Baltimore)" title="Hippodrome Theatre (Baltimore)">Hippodrome Theatre</a> in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Reginald_F._Lewis_Museum_of_Maryland_African_American_History_%26_Culture" title="Reginald F. Lewis Museum of Maryland African American History &amp; Culture">Reginald F. Lewis Museum of Maryland African American History &amp; Culture</a> in 2005, and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Slavic_Museum" title="National Slavic Museum">National Slavic Museum</a> in 2012. On April 12, 2012, Johns Hopkins held a dedication ceremony to mark the completion of one of the United States' largest medical complexes – the Johns Hopkins Hospital in Baltimore – which features the Sheikh Zayed Cardiovascular and Critical Care Tower and The Charlotte R. Bloomberg Children's Center. The event, held at the entrance to the $1.1 billion 1.6 million-square-foot-facility, honored the many donors including <a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Sultan_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan">Sheikh Khalifa bin Zayed Al Nahyan</a>, first president of the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bloomberg" title="Michael Bloomberg">Michael Bloomberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 19, 2016 the Baltimore City Council approved a $660 million bond deal for the $5.5 billion <a href="/wiki/Port_Covington" title="Port Covington">Port Covington</a> redevelopment project championed by <a href="/wiki/Under_Armour" title="Under Armour">Under Armour</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Plank" title="Kevin Plank">Kevin Plank</a> and his real estate company Sagamore Development. Port Covington surpassed the Harbor Point development as the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax-increment_financing" title="Tax-increment financing">tax-increment financing</a> deal in Baltimore's history and among the largest urban redevelopment projects in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The waterfront development that includes the new headquarters for Under Armour, as well as shops, housing, offices, and manufacturing spaces is projected to create 26,500 permanent jobs with a $4.3 billion annual economic impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Baltimore is in north-central Maryland on the <a href="/wiki/Patapsco_River" title="Patapsco River">Patapsco River</a> close to where it empties into the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a>. The city is also located on the <a href="/wiki/Fall_line" title="Fall line">fall line</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States)" title="Piedmont (United States)">Piedmont</a> Plateau and the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_coastal_plain" title="Atlantic coastal plain">Atlantic Coastal Plain</a>, which divides Baltimore into "lower city" and "upper city". The city's elevation ranges from sea level at the harbor to 480 feet (150 m) in the northwest corner near <a href="/wiki/Pimlico,_Baltimore" title="Pimlico, Baltimore">Pimlico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elevation_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-elevation-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the 2010 Census, the city has a total area of 92.1 square miles (239 km<sup>2</sup>), of which 80.9 sq mi (210 km<sup>2</sup>) is land and 11.1 sq mi (29 km<sup>2</sup>) is water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gazetteer_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-gazetteer-4">[4]</a></sup> The total area is 12.1 percent water.</p>
<p>Baltimore is almost completely surrounded by Baltimore County, but is <a href="/wiki/Independent_city_(United_States)" title="Independent city (United States)">politically independent</a> of it. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Anne_Arundel_County,_Maryland" title="Anne Arundel County, Maryland">Anne Arundel County</a> to the south.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cityscape">Cityscape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cityscape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1108px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Baltimore exhibits examples from each period of architecture over more than two centuries, and work from many famous architects such as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Henry_Latrobe" title="Benjamin Henry Latrobe">Benjamin Latrobe</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_A._Frederick" title="George A. Frederick">George A. Frederick</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Russell_Pope" title="John Russell Pope">John Russell Pope</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mies_van_der_Rohe" title="Mies van der Rohe">Mies van der Rohe</a> and <a href="/wiki/I._M._Pei" title="I. M. Pei">I. M. Pei</a>.</p>
<p>The city is rich in architecturally significant buildings in a variety of styles. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_the_National_Shrine_of_the_Assumption_of_the_Blessed_Virgin_Mary" title="Basilica of the National Shrine of the Assumption of the Blessed Virgin Mary">Baltimore Basilica</a> (1806–1821) is a neoclassical design by Benjamin Latrobe, and also the oldest Catholic cathedral in the United States. In 1813 Robert Cary Long, Sr., built for <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_Peale" title="Rembrandt Peale">Rembrandt Peale</a> the first substantial structure in the United States designed expressly as a museum. Restored, it is now the Municipal Museum of Baltimore, or popularly the <a href="/wiki/Peale_Museum" title="Peale Museum">Peale Museum</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/McKim%27s_School" title="McKim's School">McKim Free School</a> was founded and endowed by John McKim, although the building was erected by his son <a href="/wiki/Isaac_McKim" title="Isaac McKim">Isaac</a> in 1822 after a design by William Howard and William Small. It reflects the popular interest in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a> when the nation was securing its independence, as well as a scholarly interest in recently published drawings of Athenian antiquities.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Shot_Tower" title="Phoenix Shot Tower">Phoenix Shot Tower</a> (1828), at 234.25 feet (71.40 m) tall, was the tallest building in the United States until the time of the Civil War, and is one of few remaining structures of its kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> It was constructed without the use of exterior scaffolding. The Sun Iron Building, designed by R.C. Hatfield in 1851, was the city's first iron-front building and was a model for a whole generation of downtown buildings. <a href="/wiki/Brown_Memorial_Presbyterian_Church" title="Brown Memorial Presbyterian Church">Brown Memorial Presbyterian Church</a>, built in 1870 in memory of financier <a href="/wiki/George_Brown_(financier)" title="George Brown (financier)">George Brown</a>, has <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> windows by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Comfort_Tiffany" title="Louis Comfort Tiffany">Louis Comfort Tiffany</a> and has been called "one of the most significant buildings in this city, a treasure of art and architecture" by <i>Baltimore Magazine</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun2003_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun2003-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1845 <a href="/wiki/Greek_Revival_architecture" title="Greek Revival architecture">Greek Revival</a>-style <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Street_Synagogue" title="Lloyd Street Synagogue">Lloyd Street Synagogue</a> is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oldest_synagogues_in_the_United_States" title="Oldest synagogues in the United States">oldest synagogues in the United States</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Hospital" title="Johns Hopkins Hospital">Johns Hopkins Hospital</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/John_Shaw_Billings" title="John Shaw Billings">Lt. Col. John S. Billings</a> in 1876, was a considerable achievement for its day in functional arrangement and fireproofing.</p>
<p>I.M. Pei's <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_World_Trade_Center" title="Baltimore World Trade Center">World Trade Center</a> (1977) is the tallest equilateral pentagonal building in the world at 405 feet (123 m) tall.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_Harbor_East" title="Inner Harbor East">Inner Harbor East</a> area has seen the addition of two new towers which have completed construction: a 24-floor tower that is the new world headquarters of <a href="/wiki/Legg_Mason" title="Legg Mason">Legg Mason</a>, and a 21-floor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Seasons_Hotel" title="Four Seasons Hotel">Four Seasons Hotel</a> complex.</p>
<p>The streets of Baltimore are organized in a <a href="/wiki/Grid_plan" title="Grid plan">grid pattern</a>, lined with tens of thousands of brick and <a href="/wiki/Formstone" title="Formstone">formstone</a>-faced <a href="/wiki/Terraced_house" title="Terraced house">rowhouses</a>. In <i>The Baltimore Rowhouse</i>, Mary Ellen Hayward and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Belfoure" title="Charles Belfoure">Charles Belfoure</a> considered the rowhouse as the architectural form defining Baltimore as "perhaps no other American city."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In the mid-1790s, developers began building entire neighborhoods of the British-style rowhouses, which became the dominant house type of the city early in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Formstone facings, now a common feature on Baltimore rowhouses, were an addition patented in 1937 by Albert Knight. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Waters_(director_born_1946)" title="John Waters (director born 1946)">John Waters</a> characterized formstone as "the polyester of brick" in a 30-minute documentary film, <i>Little Castles: A Formstone Phenomenon</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Oriole Park at Camden Yards is considered by many to be the most beautiful <a href="/wiki/Baseball_park" title="Baseball park">baseball park</a> in Major League Baseball, and has inspired many other cities to build their own versions of this <a href="/wiki/Retro_style" title="Retro style">retro style</a> ballpark. Camden Yards along with the National Aquarium have helped revive the Inner Harbor from what once was an <a href="/wiki/Industrial_district" title="Industrial district">industrial district</a> full of dilapidated warehouses into a bustling commercial district full of bars, restaurants and retail establishments. Today, the Inner Harbor boasts the highest, most desirable real estate in the Mid-Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>After an international competition, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Baltimore_School_of_Law" title="University of Baltimore School of Law">University of Baltimore School of Law</a> awarded the German firm <a href="/wiki/Behnisch_Architekten" title="Behnisch Architekten">Behnisch Architekten</a> 1st prize for its design, which was selected for the school's new home. After the building's opening in 2013, the design won additional honors including an ENR National "Best of the Best" Award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore's newly rehabilitated <a href="/wiki/Everyman_Theatre,_Baltimore" title="Everyman Theatre, Baltimore">Everyman Theatre</a> was honored by the Baltimore Heritage at the 2013 Preservation Awards Celebration in 2013. Everyman Theatre will receive an Adaptive Reuse and Compatible Design Award as part of Baltimore Heritage's 2013 historic preservation awards ceremony. Baltimore Heritage is Baltimore's nonprofit historic and architectural preservation organization, which works to preserve and promote Baltimore's historic buildings and neighborhoods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
