<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public">Public</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Public">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_Community_College" title="Baltimore City Community College">Baltimore City Community College</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coppin_State_University" title="Coppin State University">Coppin State University</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Morgan_State_University" title="Morgan State University">Morgan State University</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Baltimore" title="University of Baltimore">University of Baltimore</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland,_Baltimore" title="University of Maryland, Baltimore">University of Maryland, Baltimore</a></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_and_secondary_schools">Primary and secondary schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Primary and secondary schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The city's public schools are managed by <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_Public_Schools" title="Baltimore City Public Schools">Baltimore City Public Schools</a> and include schools that have been well known in the area: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carver_Vocational_Technical_High_School" title="Carver Vocational Technical High School">Carver Vocational-Technical High School</a>, the first African American vocational high school and center that was established in the state of Maryland; <a href="/wiki/Digital_Harbor_High_School" title="Digital Harbor High School">Digital Harbor High School</a>, one of the secondary schools that emphasizes <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">information technology</a>; <a href="/wiki/Lake_Clifton_Eastern_High_School" title="Lake Clifton Eastern High School">Lake Clifton Eastern High School</a>, which is the largest school campus in Baltimore City of physical size; the historic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass_Senior_High_School_(Baltimore,_Maryland)" title="Frederick Douglass Senior High School (Baltimore, Maryland)">Frederick Douglass High School</a>, which is the second oldest African American high school in the United States;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_College" title="Baltimore City College">Baltimore City College</a>, the third oldest public high school in the country;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[231]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_High_School_(Baltimore,_Maryland)" title="Western High School (Baltimore, Maryland)">Western High School</a>, the oldest public all-girls school in the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[232]</a></sup> Baltimore City College (also known as "City") and <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Baltimore Polytechnic Institute">Baltimore Polytechnic Institute</a> (also known as "Poly") share the nation's second-oldest high school <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_City_College_football" title="Baltimore City College football">football rivalry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[233]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_private_and_parochial_schools_in_Baltimore" title="List of private and parochial schools in Baltimore">List of private and parochial schools in Baltimore</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads_and_highways">Roads and highways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Roads and highways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BaltimoreLightRail.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/BaltimoreLightRail.JPG/250px-BaltimoreLightRail.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/BaltimoreLightRail.JPG/375px-BaltimoreLightRail.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/BaltimoreLightRail.JPG/500px-BaltimoreLightRail.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BaltimoreLightRail.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore_Light_Rail" title="Baltimore Light Rail">Baltimore Light Rail</a> provides service to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_International_Thurgood_Marshall_Airport" title="Baltimore–Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport">Baltimore–Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport</a> and the Baltimore area. Here, a train stops at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_Center_(Baltimore_Light_Rail_station)" title="Convention Center (Baltimore Light Rail station)">Convention Center (Baltimore Light Rail station)</a>, just west of the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Convention_Center" title="Baltimore Convention Center">Baltimore Convention Center</a> on <a href="/wiki/Pratt_Street" title="Pratt Street">Pratt Street</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate highways</a> serving Baltimore are <a href="/wiki/Interstate_70_in_Maryland" title="Interstate 70 in Maryland">I-70</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interstate_83" title="Interstate 83">I-83</a> (the Jones Falls Expressway), <a href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_Maryland" title="Interstate 95 in Maryland">I-95</a> (the John F. Kennedy Memorial Highway north of the city), <a href="/wiki/Interstate_395_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 395 (Maryland)">I-395</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interstate_695_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 695 (Maryland)">I-695</a> (the Baltimore Beltway), <a href="/wiki/Interstate_795_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 795 (Maryland)">I-795</a> (the Northwest Expressway), <a href="/wiki/Interstate_895_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 895 (Maryland)">I-895</a> (the Harbor Tunnel Thruway), and <a href="/wiki/Interstate_97" title="Interstate 97">I-97</a>. The city's mainline Interstate highways—I-95, I-83, and I-70—do not directly connect to each other, and in the case of I-70 end at a park and ride lot just inside the city limits, because of <a href="/wiki/Highway_revolts" title="Highway revolts">freeway revolts</a> in Baltimore. These revolts were led primarily by <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Mikulski" title="Barbara Mikulski">Barbara Mikulski</a>, a former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_senator" title="United States senator">United States senator</a> for Maryland, which resulted in the abandonment of the original plan. There are two tunnels traversing Baltimore Harbor within the city limits: the four-bore <a href="/wiki/Fort_McHenry_Tunnel" title="Fort McHenry Tunnel">Fort McHenry Tunnel</a> (serving <a href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_Maryland" title="Interstate 95 in Maryland">I-95</a>) and the two-bore <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Harbor_Tunnel" title="Baltimore Harbor Tunnel">Harbor Tunnel</a> (serving I-895). The Baltimore Beltway crosses south of Baltimore Harbor over the <a href="/wiki/Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_(Baltimore)" title="Francis Scott Key Bridge (Baltimore)">Francis Scott Key Bridge</a>.</p>
<p>The only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Numbered_Highways" title="United States Numbered Highways">U.S. Highways</a> in the city are <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_1_in_Maryland" title="U.S. Route 1 in Maryland">US 1</a>, which bypasses downtown, and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_40_in_Maryland" title="U.S. Route 40 in Maryland">US 40</a>, which crosses downtown from east to west. Both run along major surface streets; however, US 40 utilizes a small section of a freeway cancelled in the 1970s in the west side of the city originally intended for <a href="/wiki/Interstate_170_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 170 (Maryland)">Interstate 170</a>. State routes in the city also travel along surface streets, with the exception of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maryland_Route_295" title="Maryland Route 295">Maryland Route 295</a>, which carries the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_Parkway" title="Baltimore–Washington Parkway">Baltimore–Washington Parkway</a>.</p>
<p>The Baltimore City Department of Transportation (BCDOT) is responsible for several functions of the road transportation system in Baltimore, including repairing roads, sidewalks, and alleys; road signs; street lights; and managing the flow of transportation systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcdot_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcdot-237">[234]</a></sup> In addition, the agency is in charge of vehicle towing and <a href="/wiki/Traffic_camera" title="Traffic camera">traffic cameras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcdot2_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcdot2-238">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcdot3_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcdot3-239">[236]</a></sup> BCDOT maintains all streets within the city of Baltimore. These include all streets that are marked as state and U.S. highways as well as the portions of I-83 and I-70 within the city limits. The only highways within the city that are not maintained by BCDOT are I-95, I-395, I-695, and I-895; those four highways are maintained by the Maryland Transportation Authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baltimore_City_HLR_2005_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baltimore_City_HLR_2005-240">[237]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transit_systems">Transit systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Transit systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_transit">Public transit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Public transit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="613" data-file-width="800" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg/220px-Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg/330px-Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg/440px-Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charm_City_Circulator_Vanhool_1101.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Charm City Circulator Van Hool A330 #1101 on the Orange Line</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Public transit in Baltimore is mostly provided by the <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Transit_Administration" title="Maryland Transit Administration">Maryland Transit Administration</a> (abbreviated "MTA Maryland") and <a href="/wiki/Charm_City_Circulator" title="Charm City Circulator">Charm City Circulator</a>. MTA Maryland operates a comprehensive <a href="/wiki/MTA_Maryland_bus_service" title="MTA Maryland bus service">bus</a> network, including many local, express, and commuter buses, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore_Light_Rail" title="Baltimore Light Rail">a light rail network</a> connecting <a href="/wiki/Hunt_Valley,_Maryland" title="Hunt Valley, Maryland">Hunt Valley</a> in the north to BWI Airport and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cromwell_Station_/_Glen_Burnie_(Baltimore_Light_Rail_station)" title="Cromwell Station / Glen Burnie (Baltimore Light Rail station)">Cromwell</a> (<a href="/wiki/Glen_Burnie,_Maryland" title="Glen Burnie, Maryland">Glen Burnie</a>) in the south, and a <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Metro_Subway" title="Baltimore Metro Subway">subway line</a> between <a href="/wiki/Owings_Mills,_Maryland" title="Owings Mills, Maryland">Owings Mills</a> and Johns Hopkins Hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[238]</a></sup> A proposed rail line, known as the <a href="/wiki/Red_Line_(Baltimore)" title="Red Line (Baltimore)">Red Line</a>, which would link the Social Security Administration to <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Bayview_Medical_Center" title="Johns Hopkins Bayview Medical Center">Johns Hopkins Bayview Medical Center</a> and perhaps the <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Baltimore" title="Canton, Baltimore">Canton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dundalk,_Maryland" title="Dundalk, Maryland">Dundalk</a> communities, was cancelled as of June 2015 by Governor <a href="/wiki/Larry_Hogan" title="Larry Hogan">Larry Hogan</a>; a proposal to extend Baltimore's existing subway line to Morgan State University, known as the <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_(Baltimore)" title="Green Line (Baltimore)">Green Line</a>, is in the planning stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[239]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Charm City Circulator (CCC), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shuttle_bus_service" title="Shuttle bus service">shuttle bus service</a> operated by Veolia Transportation for the Baltimore Department of Transportation, began operating in the downtown area in January 2010. Funded partly by a 16 percent increase in the city's parking fees, the circulator provides free bus service seven days a week, picking up passengers every 15 minutes at designated stops during service hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veolia_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Veolia-243">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC_City_Paper_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-CCC_City_Paper-244">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>The CCC's first bus line, the Orange route, travels between Hollins Market and Harbor East. Its Purple route, launched June 7, 2010, operates between Penn Station and Federal Hill. The Green route runs between Johns Hopkins and City Hall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CCC_City_Paper_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-CCC_City_Paper-244">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup> The Charm City Circulator operates a fleet of diesel and hybrid vehicles built by DesignLine, Orion, and Van Hool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Veolia_243-1"><a href="#cite_note-Veolia-243">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>Baltimore also has a <a href="/wiki/Water_taxi" title="Water taxi">water taxi</a> service, operated by <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Water_Taxi" title="Baltimore Water Taxi">Baltimore Water Taxi</a>. The water taxi's six routes provide service throughout the city's harbor, and was purchased by <a href="/wiki/Under_Armour" title="Under Armour">Under Armour</a> CEO <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Plank" title="Kevin Plank">Kevin Plank</a>'s Sagamore Ventures in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2017, The BaltimoreLink started operating; it is the redesign of the region's initial bus system. The BaltimoreLink runs through downtown Baltimore every 10 minutes via color-coded, high-frequency CityLink routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[244]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Intercity_rail">Intercity rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Intercity rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2328" data-file-width="2790" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg/220px-Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg/330px-Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg/440px-Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baltimore_Pennsylvania_Station_corrected.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(Baltimore)" title="Pennsylvania Station (Baltimore)">Baltimore Pennsylvania Station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baltimore is a top destination for <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a>. Baltimore's <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(Baltimore)" title="Pennsylvania Station (Baltimore)">Penn Station</a> is one of the busiest in the country. In FY 2014, Penn Station was ranked the <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_Amtrak_stations" title="List of busiest Amtrak stations">seventh-busiest</a> rail station in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> by number of passengers served each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fact_Sheet_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fact_Sheet-248">[245]</a></sup> The building sits on a raised "island" of sorts between two open trenches, one for the Jones Falls Expressway and the other for the tracks of the Northeast Corridor (NEC). The NEC approaches from the south through the two-track, 7,660-foot <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_and_Potomac_Tunnel" title="Baltimore and Potomac Tunnel">Baltimore and Potomac Tunnel</a>, which opened in 1873 and whose 30 mph limit, sharp curves, and steep grades make it one of the NEC's worst bottlenecks. The NEC's northern approach is the 1873 <a href="/wiki/Union_Tunnel_(Baltimore)" title="Union Tunnel (Baltimore)">Union Tunnel</a>, which has one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_track_(rail)" title="Single track (rail)">single-track</a> bore and one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_track" title="Double track">double-track</a> bore.</p>
<p>Just outside the city, <a href="/wiki/BWI_Rail_Station" title="BWI Rail Station">Baltimore/Washington International (BWI) Thurgood Marshall Airport Rail Station</a> is another popular stop. Amtrak's <i><a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">Acela Express</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Palmetto_(train)" title="Palmetto (train)">Palmetto</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Carolinian_(train)" title="Carolinian (train)">Carolinian</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Silver_Star_(Amtrak_train)" title="Silver Star (Amtrak train)">Silver Star</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Silver_Meteor" title="Silver Meteor">Silver Meteor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vermonter_(train)" title="Vermonter (train)">Vermonter</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crescent_(train)" title="Crescent (train)">Crescent</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Regional" title="Northeast Regional">Northeast Regional</a></i> trains are the scheduled passenger train services that stop in the city. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/MARC_Train" title="MARC Train">MARC</a> commuter rail service connects the city's two main intercity rail stations, Camden Station and Penn Station, with Washington, D.C.'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">Union Station</a> as well as stops in between. The MARC consists of 3 lines; the Brunswick, Camden and Penn. On December 7, 2013 the Penn Line began weekend service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airports">Airports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Airports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kbwi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kbwi.jpg/250px-Kbwi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kbwi.jpg/375px-Kbwi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kbwi.jpg/500px-Kbwi.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kbwi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The interior of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_International_Thurgood_Marshall_Airport" title="Baltimore–Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport">Baltimore–Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport</a>, Baltimore's major commercial airport</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Baltimore is served by two airports, both operated by the Maryland Aviation Administration, which is part of the <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Department_of_Transportation" title="Maryland Department of Transportation">Maryland Department of Transportation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[247]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_International_Thurgood_Marshall_Airport" title="Baltimore–Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport">Baltimore–Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport</a>, generally known as "BWI," lies about 10 miles (16 km) to the south of Baltimore in neighboring <a href="/wiki/Anne_Arundel_County,_Maryland" title="Anne Arundel County, Maryland">Anne Arundel County</a>. The airport is named after <a href="/wiki/Thurgood_Marshall" title="Thurgood Marshall">Thurgood Marshall</a>, a Baltimore native who was the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> to serve on the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>. In terms of passenger traffic, BWI is the 22nd busiest airport in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[248]</a></sup> As of calendar year 2014, BWI is the largest, by passenger count, of three major airports serving the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_Metropolitan_Area" title="Baltimore–Washington Metropolitan Area">Baltimore–Washington Metropolitan Area</a>. It is accessible by I-95 and the Baltimore–Washington Parkway via <a href="/wiki/Interstate_195_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 195 (Maryland)">Interstate 195</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore_Light_Rail" title="Baltimore Light Rail">Baltimore Light Rail</a>, and Amtrak and MARC Train at <a href="/wiki/BWI_Rail_Station" title="BWI Rail Station">BWI Rail Station</a>.</p>
<p>Baltimore is also served by <a href="/wiki/Martin_State_Airport" title="Martin State Airport">Martin State Airport</a>, a <a href="/wiki/General_aviation" title="General aviation">general aviation</a> facility, to the northeast in Baltimore County. Martin State Airport is linked to <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Baltimore" title="Downtown Baltimore">downtown Baltimore</a> by <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Route_150" title="Maryland Route 150">Maryland Route 150</a> (Eastern Avenue) and by MARC Train at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_State_Airport_(MARC_station)" title="Martin State Airport (MARC station)">its own station</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pedestrians_and_bicycles">Pedestrians and bicycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Pedestrians and bicycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Baltimore has a comprehensive system of bicycle routes in the city. These routes are not numbered, but are typically denoted with green signs sporting a silhouette of a bicycle upon an outline of the city's border, and denote the distance to destinations, much like bicycle routes in the rest of the state. The roads carrying bicycle routes are also labelled with either bike lanes, sharrows, or Share the Road signs. Many of these routes pass through the downtown area. The network of bicycle lanes in the city continues to expand, with over 140 miles added between 2006 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup> Alongside bike lanes, Baltimore has also built <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bike_boulevard" title="Bike boulevard">bike boulevards</a>, starting with Guilford Avenue in 2012.</p>
<p>Baltimore currently has three major trail systems within the city. The <a href="/wiki/Gwynns_Falls_Trail" title="Gwynns Falls Trail">Gwynns Falls Trail</a> runs from the Inner Harbor to the I-70 Park and Ride, passing through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gwynns_Falls_Park" title="Gwynns Falls Park">Gwynns Falls Park</a> and possessing numerous branches. There are also many pedestrian hiking trails traversing the park. The <a href="/wiki/Jones_Falls_Trail" title="Jones Falls Trail">Jones Falls Trail</a> currently runs from the Inner Harbor to the Cylburn Arboretum; however, it is currently undergoing expansion. Long term plans call for it to extend to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Washington_(Baltimore_Light_Rail_station)" title="Mount Washington (Baltimore Light Rail station)">Mount Washington Light Rail Stop</a>, and possibly as far north as the Falls Road stop to connect to the Robert E. Lee boardwalk north of the city. It will also incorporate a spur alongside Western Run. The two aforementioned trails carry sections of the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Greenway" title="East Coast Greenway">East Coast Greenway</a> through the city. There is also the Herring Run Trail, which runs from <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Route_147" title="Maryland Route 147">Harford Road</a> east to its end beyond Sinclair Lane, utilizing Herring Run Park; long term plans also call for its extension to Morgan State University and north to points beyond. Other major bicycle projects include a protected cycle track installed on both Maryland Avenue and Mount Royal Avenue, expected to become the backbone of a downtown bicycle network. Installation for the cycletracks is expected in 2014 and 2016, respectively.</p>
<p>In addition to the bicycle trails and cycletracks, Baltimore has the Stony Run Trail, a walking path that will eventually connect from the Jones Falls north to Northern Parkway, utilizing much of the old Ma and Pa Railroad corridor inside the city. In 2011, the city undertook a campaign to reconstruct many sidewalk ramps in the city, coinciding with mass resurfacing of the city's streets. A 2011 study by <a href="/wiki/Walk_Score" title="Walk Score">Walk Score</a> ranked Baltimore the 14th most walkable of fifty largest U.S. cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[250]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Port_of_Baltimore">Port of Baltimore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Port of Baltimore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helen_Delich_Bentley_Port_of_Baltimore" title="Helen Delich Bentley Port of Baltimore">Helen Delich Bentley Port of Baltimore</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baltimoreharborview.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Baltimoreharborview.jpg/220px-Baltimoreharborview.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Baltimoreharborview.jpg/330px-Baltimoreharborview.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Baltimoreharborview.jpg/440px-Baltimoreharborview.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

Eastward view Baltimore's <a href="/wiki/Inner_Harbor" title="Inner Harbor">Inner Harbor</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Washington_Monument,_1849,_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="582" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Washington_Monument%2C_1849%2C_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg/250px-Washington_Monument%2C_1849%2C_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Washington_Monument%2C_1849%2C_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg/375px-Washington_Monument%2C_1849%2C_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Washington_Monument%2C_1849%2C_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg/500px-Washington_Monument%2C_1849%2C_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Washington_Monument,_1849,_from_Federal_Hill_1a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Baltimore harbor in 1849 with the prominent <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument_(Baltimore)" title="Washington Monument (Baltimore)">Washington Monument</a> in the background north of the city</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_(Baltimore).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3321" data-file-width="4981" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_%28Baltimore%29.jpg/250px-The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_%28Baltimore%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_%28Baltimore%29.jpg/375px-The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_%28Baltimore%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_%28Baltimore%29.jpg/500px-The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_%28Baltimore%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_(Baltimore).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Francis_Scott_Key_Bridge_(Baltimore)" title="Francis Scott Key Bridge (Baltimore)">Francis Scott Key Bridge</a> over the Baltimore harbor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The port was founded in 1706, preceding the founding of Baltimore. The Maryland colonial legislature made the area near <a href="/wiki/Locust_Point,_Baltimore" title="Locust Point, Baltimore">Locust Point</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_entry" title="Port of entry">port of entry</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a> trade with England. Fells Point, the deepest point in the natural harbor, soon became the colony's main ship building center, later on becoming leader in the construction of <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Clipper" title="Baltimore Clipper">clipper ships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Baltimore's founding, mills were built behind the wharves. The <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a> led to many orders for fast vessels; many overland pioneers also relied upon canned goods from Baltimore. After the Civil War, a coffee ship was designed here for trade with <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. At the end of the nineteenth century, European ship lines had terminals for immigrants. The Baltimore and Ohio Railroad made the port a major <a href="/wiki/Transshipment" title="Transshipment">transshipment</a> point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stover_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-255">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17,75</sup> Currently the port has major <a href="/wiki/Roll-on/roll-off" title="Roll-on/roll-off">roll-on/roll-off</a> facilities, as well as bulk facilities, especially steel handling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[253]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_taxi" title="Water taxi">Water taxis</a> also operate in the Inner Harbor. Governor Ehrlich participated in naming the port after <a href="/wiki/Helen_Delich_Bentley" title="Helen Delich Bentley">Helen Delich Bentley</a> during the 300th anniversary of the port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, Duke Realty Corporation began a new development near the Port of Baltimore, named the Chesapeake Commerce Center. This new industrial park is located on the site of a former General Motors plant. The total project comprises 184 acres (0.74 km<sup>2</sup>) in eastern Baltimore City, and the site will yield 2,800,000 square feet (260,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of warehouse/distribution and office space. Chesapeake Commerce Center has direct access to two major Interstate highways (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-95" title="I-95">I-95</a> and <a href="/wiki/Interstate_895_(Maryland)" title="Interstate 895 (Maryland)">I-895</a>) and is located adjacent to two of the major Port of Baltimore terminals. The Port of Baltimore is one of two seaports on the U.S. East Coast with a 50-foot (15 m) dredge to accommodate the largest shipping vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>Along with cargo terminals, the port also has a passenger cruise terminal, which offers year-round trips on several lines, including Royal Caribbean's Grandeur of the Seas and Carnival's Pride. Overall five cruise lines have operated out of the port to the Bahamas and the Caribbean, while some ships traveled to New England and Canada. The terminal has become a popular embarkation point where passengers have the rare opportunity to park and board next to the ship visible from Interstate 95.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup> Passengers from Pennsylvania, New York and New Jersey make up a third of the volume, with travelers from Maryland, Virginia, the District and even Ohio and the Carolinas making up the rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Baltimore's Inner Harbor, known for its skyline waterscape and its tourist-friendly areas, was horribly polluted. The waterway was often filled with garbage after heavy rainstorms, failing its 2014 water quality report card. The Waterfront Partnership of Baltimore took steps to remediate the waterways, in hopes that the harbor would be fishable and swimmable once again.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trash_interceptors">Trash interceptors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Trash interceptors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1446" data-file-width="1802" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg/220px-Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg/330px-Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg/440px-Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mr._Trash_Wheel--Baltimore_MD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "Mr. Trash Wheel" <a href="/wiki/Trash_interceptor" title="Trash interceptor">trash interceptor</a> at the mouth of the Jones Falls River in Baltimore's <a href="/wiki/Inner_Harbor" title="Inner Harbor">Inner Harbor</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trash_interceptor#Baltimore.27s_Mr._Trash_Wheel" title="Trash interceptor">Trash interceptor § Baltimore's Mr. Trash Wheel</a></div>
<p>Installed in May 2014, the water wheel trash interceptor known as Mr. Trash Wheel sits at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Jones_Falls" title="Jones Falls">Jones Falls</a> River in Baltimore's Inner Harbor. A February 2015 agreement with a local waste-to-energy plant is believed to make Baltimore the first city to use reclaimed waterway debris to generate electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mr. Trash Wheel is the world's first permanent water wheel trash interceptor to clean up the city's polluted Inner Harbor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ew20151217_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-ew20151217-262">[259]</a></sup> The Jones Falls river watershed drains fifty-eight square miles of land outside of Baltimore and is a significant source of trash that enters the harbor. Garbage collected by Mr. Trash Wheel could come from anywhere in the Jones Falls Watershed area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[260]</a></sup> The wheel moves continuously, removing garbage and dumping it into an attached dumpster using only hydro and solar renewable power to keep its wheel turning. It has the capability to collect 50,000 pounds of trash per day, and has removed more than 350 tons of litter from Baltimore's landmark and tourist attraction in its first 18 months, estimated as consisting of approximately 200,000 bottles, 173,000 potato chip bags and 6.7 million cigarettes butts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[262]</a></sup> The Water Wheel has been very successful at trash removal, visibly decreasing the amount of garbage that collects in the harbor, especially after a rainfall.</p>
<p>After the success of Mr. Trash Wheel, the Waterfront Partnership raised money to build a second Water Wheel at the end of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Harris_Creek_(Baltimore)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Harris Creek (Baltimore) (page does not exist)">Harris Creek</a>, an entirely piped stream that flows beneath Baltimore's <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Baltimore" title="Canton, Baltimore">Canton</a> neighborhood and empties into the Baltimore Harbor. Harris Creek is known to carry tons of trash every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[265]</a></sup> The planned new Water Wheel was inaugurated in December 2016, and dubbed "Professor Trash Wheel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[266]</a></sup> Professor Trash Wheel prevents waste from exiting the Harbor and accessing the Chesapeake Bay and Atlantic Ocean. A number of additional projects are going on in Baltimore City and County that should result in better water quality scores. These projects include the Blue Alleys project, expanded street sweeping, and stream restoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ew20151217_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-ew20151217-262">[259]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_water_pollution_control">Other water pollution control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Other water pollution control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In August 2010, the National Aquarium assembled, planted, and launched a <a href="/wiki/Floating_island" title="Floating island">floating wetland island</a> designed by Biohabitats in Baltimore's Inner Harbor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup> Hundreds of years ago Baltimore's harbor shoreline would have been lined with tidal <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>. Floating wetlands provide many environmental benefits to water quality and habitat enhancement, which is why the Waterfront Partnership of Baltimore has included them in their Healthy Harbor Initiative pilot projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[268]</a></sup> Biohabitats also developed a concept to transform a dilapidated wharf into a living pier that cleans Harbor water, provides habitat and is an aesthetic attraction. Currently under design, the top of the pier will become a <a href="/wiki/Constructed_wetland" title="Constructed wetland">constructed tidal wetland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_in_Baltimore" title="Media in Baltimore">Media in Baltimore</a></div>
<p>Baltimore's main newspaper is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Baltimore_Sun" title="The Baltimore Sun">The Baltimore Sun</a></i>. It was sold by its Baltimore owners in 1986 to the Times Mirror Company,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[270]</a></sup> which was bought by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribune_Company" title="Tribune Company">Tribune Company</a> in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[271]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Baltimore_News-American" title="Baltimore News-American">Baltimore News-American</a></i>, another long-running paper that competed with the Sun, ceased publication in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[272]</a></sup></p>
<p>The city is home to the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Afro-American" title="Baltimore Afro-American">Baltimore Afro-American</a>, an influential African American newspaper founded in 1892.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McEwen_2012_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-McEwen_2012-277">[274]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Baltimore_Examiner" title="The Baltimore Examiner">The Baltimore Examiner</a></i> was launched to compete with <i>The Sun</i>. It was part of a national chain that includes <i><a href="/wiki/The_San_Francisco_Examiner" title="The San Francisco Examiner">The San Francisco Examiner</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Examiner" title="The Washington Examiner">The Washington Examiner</a></i>. In contrast to the paid subscription <i>Sun</i>, <i>The Examiner</i> was a free newspaper funded solely by advertisements. Unable to turn a profit and facing a deep recession, <i>The Baltimore Examiner</i> ceased publication on February 15, 2009.</p>
<p>Despite being located 40 miles northeast of <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, Baltimore is a major media market in its own right, with all major English language television networks represented in the city. <a href="/wiki/WJZ-TV" title="WJZ-TV">WJZ-TV</a> is a <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Owned_and_operated_station" title="Owned and operated station">owned and operated station</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WBFF" title="WBFF">WBFF</a> is the flagship of <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Broadcast_Group" title="Sinclair Broadcast Group">Sinclair Broadcast Group</a>, the largest station owner in the country.</p>
<p>Nielsen ranked Baltimore as the 26th-largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_market" title="Television market">television market</a> for the 2008–2009 viewing season and the 27th-largest for 2009–2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[275]</a></sup> Arbitron's Fall 2010 rankings identified Baltimore as the 22nd largest radio market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[276]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people">Notable people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: Notable people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_from_Baltimore" title="List of people from Baltimore">List of people from Baltimore</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sister_cities">Sister cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: Sister cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Baltimore has ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twin_towns_and_sister_cities" title="Twin towns and sister cities">sister cities</a>, as designated by <a href="/wiki/Sister_Cities_International" title="Sister Cities International">Sister Cities International</a>: <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[278]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel"><img alt="Israel" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="660" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Flag_of_Israel.svg/21px-Flag_of_Israel.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Flag_of_Israel.svg/32px-Flag_of_Israel.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Flag_of_Israel.svg/41px-Flag_of_Israel.svg.png 2x" width="21"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">Ashkelon</a>, Israel (2005)</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Baltimore&amp;action=edit&amp;section=74" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Officially, seasonal snowfall accumulation has ranged from 0.7 in (1.8 cm) in 1949–50 to 77.0 in (196 cm) in 2009–10. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_American_blizzard_of_2009#Snowfall" title="North American blizzard of 2009">North American blizzard of 2009#Snowfall</a> (December 19–20, 2009), <a href="/wiki/February_5%E2%80%936,_2010_North_American_blizzard#Snowfall" title="February 5–6, 2010 North American blizzard">February 5–6, 2010 North American blizzard#Snowfall</a>, and <a href="/wiki/February_9%E2%80%9310,_2010_North_American_blizzard#Impact" title="February 9–10, 2010 North American blizzard">February 9–10, 2010 North American blizzard#Impact</a>. The February storms contributed to a monthly accumulation of 50.0 in (127 cm), the most for any month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_NowData_2_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA_NowData_2-123">[123]</a></sup> If no snow fell outside of February that winter, 2009–10 would still rank as 5th snowiest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since 1950, when the National Weather Service switched to using the suburban and generally much cooler BWI Airport as the official Baltimore climatology station, this extreme has repeated three times: January 29, 1963, <a href="/wiki/Cold_Sunday" title="Cold Sunday">January 17, 1982</a>, and January 22, 1984.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-134">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Temperature, precipitation normals are recorded at <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Science_Center" title="Maryland Science Center">Maryland Science Center</a> in downtown; the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> does not yet record snowfall at this location, so the snow normals for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWI_Airport" title="BWI Airport">BWI Airport</a>, at an elevation of 156 ft (47.5 m) about 10 mi (16 km) south of downtown, are shown. Likewise humidity and sun duration normals were recorded at BWI Airport.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As depicted on a map of the Piscataway lands in Kenneth Bryson, <i>Images of America: Accokeek</i> (Arcadia Publishing, 2013) pp. 10–11, derived from Alice and Henry Ferguson, <i>The Piscataway Indians of Southern Maryland</i> (Alice Ferguson Foundation, 1960) pp. 8 (map) and p. 11: "By the beginning of Maryland (English) settlement, pressure from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Susquehannocks" title="Susquehannocks">Susquehannocks</a> had reduced..the Piscataway 'empire'...to a belt bordering the Potomac south of the falls and extending up the principle tributaries. Roughly, the 'empire' covered the southern half of present Prince Georges County and all, or nearly all, of Charles County."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">William Harvey Hunter, "Baltimore Architecture in History"; in Dorsey &amp; Dilts (1997), p. 7. "Both begun in 1815, the Battle Monument and the Washington Monument gave Baltimore its most famous sobriquet. In 1827, when bremoth of them were nearly finished, President John Quincy Adams at a big public dinner in Baltimore gave as his toast, 'Baltimore, the monumental city.' It was more than an idle comment: no other large city in America had even one substantial monument to show."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-CP-homelessness-19Oct2011-157"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CP-homelessness-19Oct2011_157-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Smith, Van (October 19, 2011). <a class="external text" href="http://citypaper.com/news/census-shows-striking-growth-in-baltimore-homelessness-1.1219779" rel="nofollow">"Census shows striking growth in Baltimore homelessness Population swells nearly 20 percent in two years; ranks of homeless young people increase 50 percent"</a>. <i>CityPaper</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">August 9,</span> 2012</span>. <q>The biennial homeless censuses, which are required under federal law and are conducted on a single day—this year, Jan. 25—have trended upward since the first one in 2003 counted 2,681 homeless people in Baltimore, compared to 4,088 this year, according to the report by Morgan State's School of Architecture and Planning. Called a "point-in-time" survey, the census effort looks for homeless people living on the streets as well as those checking into shelters and hospital emergency rooms and receiving other homeless services. The count of Baltimore's young homeless people, which is evaluated separately by the Center for Adolescent Health at the Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School of Public Health and is undertaken over a period of weeks instead of one day, has risen 135 percent since 2007, from 272 to 640. Rather than canvassing the streets for homeless youngsters, the effort relies on data provided by cooperating service providers, including the city public-schools system.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABaltimore&amp;rft.atitle=Census+shows+striking+growth+in+Baltimore+homelessness+Population+swells+nearly+20+percent+in+two+years%3B+ranks+of+homeless+young+people+increase+50+percent&amp;rft.aufirst=Van&amp;rft.aulast=Smith&amp;rft.date=2011-10-19&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=CityPaper&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcitypaper.com%2Fnews%2Fcensus-shows-striking-growth-in-baltimore-homelessness-1.1219779&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

(First edition published in 1973.) <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-87033-477-8" title="Special:BookSources/0-87033-477-8">0-87033-477-8</a>.</li>
<li>Hall, Clayton Coleman (1912). <i>Baltimore: Its History and Its People</i>. New York: Lewis Historical Publishing Company. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/baltimoreitshist01hall" rel="nofollow">Vol. 1</a>.</li>
<li>Orser, Edward W. (1994). <i>Blockbusting in Baltimore: the Edmonston Village Story</i>. University Press of Kentucky. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8131-1870-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-8131-1870-0">0-8131-1870-0</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Thomas_Scharf" title="John Thomas Scharf">Scharf, J. Thomas</a> (1879). <i>History of Maryland from the Earliest Period to the Present Day.</i> Baltimore: John B. Piet. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historymaryland01thomgoog" rel="nofollow">Vol. 1</a>; <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historymaryland00thomgoog" rel="nofollow">Vol. 2</a>; <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/historymaryland02thomgoog" rel="nofollow">Vol. 3</a>.</li>
<li>Townsend, Camilla (2000). <i>Tales of Two Cities: Race and Economic Culture in Early Republican North and South America: Guyaquil, Ecuador, and Baltimore, Maryland. University of Texas Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-292-78167-9" title="Special:BookSources/0-292-78167-9">0-292-78167-9</a>.</i></li>
</ul>

<th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" scope="col">

<div id="Location_of_the_capital_of_the_United_States_and_predecessors" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><a href="/wiki/List_of_capitals_in_the_United_States" title="List of capitals in the United States">Location of the capital of the United States</a> and predecessors</div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%;text-align:left;"><span style="font-weight:normal; font-size:90%;">1774</span><span class="nowrap">   </span><a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;width:100%;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%;text-align:left;"><span style="font-weight:normal; font-size:90%;">1775–81</span><span class="nowrap">   </span><a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;width:100%;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> <big>→</big> <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Baltimore</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/York,_Pennsylvania" title="York, Pennsylvania">York</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%;text-align:left;"><span style="font-weight:normal; font-size:90%;">1781–89</span><span class="nowrap">   </span><a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;width:100%;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Annapolis,_Maryland" title="Annapolis, Maryland">Annapolis</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey" title="Trenton, New Jersey">Trenton</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a></div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%;text-align:left;"><span style="font-weight:normal; font-size:90%;">1789–present</span><span class="nowrap">   </span><a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">Federal government of the United States</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;width:100%;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em"><a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> <big>→</big> <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></div>
</td>
</tr>
